## @foundmyfitness - HIIT and Sleep Deprivation Recovery
> If you're sleep deprived, one of the best things you can do (counterintuitively) is high-intensity exercise.
>
> A few hours of sleep deprivation reduces insulin sensitivity and impairs cognitive function.
>
> But just 10 minutes of HIIT can enhance brain blood flow, cognitive performance, and blood glucose regulation. It can take you back to baseline, or even above it, for several hours or until you can get adequate sleep.
>
> You may not feel like going hard when you're tired, but the short-term benefits are real. Just don't make poor sleep a habit.

- **Tweet:** https://x.com/foundmyfitness/status/2009016116396310639
- **What:** Research-backed advice on using high-intensity interval training as a counterintuitive recovery method during sleep deprivation to restore cognitive function and metabolic markers.
## @TheAhmadOsman - LLM Engineering Projects Curriculum
> step-by-step LLM Engineering Projects
>
> each project = one concept learned the hard (i.e. real) way
>
>     Tokenization & Embeddings
>
> > build byte-pair encoder + train your own subword vocab
> > write a "token visualizer" to map words/chunks to IDs
> > one-hot vs learned-embedding: plot cosine distances
>
>     Positional Embeddings
>
> > classic sinusoidal vs learned vs RoPE vs ALiBi: demo all four
> > animate a toy sequence being "position-encoded" in 3D
> > ablate positionsâ€”watch attention collapse
>
>     Self-Attention & Multihead Attention
>
> > hand-wire dot-product attention for one token
> > scale to multi-head, plot per-head weight heatmaps
> > mask out future tokens, verify causal property
>
>     transformers, QKV, & stacking
>
> > stack the Attention implementations with LayerNorm and residuals â†’ single-block transformer
> > generalize: n-block "mini-former" on toy data
> > dissect Q, K, V: swap them, break them, see what explodes
>
>     Sampling Parameters: temp/top-k/top-p
>
> > code a sampler dashboard â€” interactively tune temp/k/p and sample outputs
> > plot entropy vs output diversity as you sweep params
> > nuke temp=0 (argmax): watch repetition
>
>     KV Cache (Fast Inference)
>
> > record & reuse KV states; measure speedup vs no-cache
> > build a "cache hit/miss" visualizer for token streams
> > profile cache memory cost for long vs short sequences
>
>     Long-Context Tricks: Infini-Attention / Sliding Window
>
> > implement sliding window attention; measure loss on long docs
> > benchmark "memory-efficient" (recompute, flash) variants
> > plot perplexity vs context length; find context collapse point
>
>     Mixture of Experts (MoE)
>
> > code a 2-expert router layer; route tokens dynamically
> > plot expert utilization histograms over dataset
> > simulate sparse/dense swaps; measure FLOP savings
>
>     Grouped Query Attention
>
> > convert your mini-former to grouped query layout
> > measure speed vs vanilla multi-head on large batch
> > ablate number of groups, plot latency
>
>     Normalization & Activations
>
> > hand-implement LayerNorm, RMSNorm, SwiGLU, GELU
> > ablate eachâ€”what happens to train/test loss?
> > plot activation distributions layerwise
>
>     Pretraining Objectives
>
> > train masked LM vs causal LM vs prefix LM on toy text
> > plot loss curves; compare which learns "English" faster
> > generate samples from each â€” note quirks
>
>     Finetuning vs Instruction Tuning vs RLHF
>
> > fine-tune on a small custom dataset
> > instruction-tune by prepending tasks ("Summarize: ...")
> > RLHF: hack a reward model, use PPO for 10 steps, plot reward
>
>     Scaling Laws & Model Capacity
>
> > train tiny, small, medium models â€” plot loss vs size
> > benchmark wall-clock time, VRAM, throughput
> > extrapolate scaling curve â€” how "dumb" can you go?
>
>     Quantization
>
> > code PTQ & QAT; export to GGUF/AWQ; plot accuracy drop
>
>     Inference/Training Stacks:
>
> > port a model from HuggingFace to Deepspeed, vLLM, ExLlama
> > profile throughput, VRAM, latency across all three
>
>     Synthetic Data
>
> > generate toy data, add noise, dedupe, create eval splits
> > visualize model learning curves on real vs synth
>
>     each project = one core insight. build. plot. break. repeat.
>
> > don't get stuck too long in theory
> > code, debug, ablate, even meme your graphs lol
> > finish each and post what you learned
>
> your future self will thank you later

- **Tweet:** https://x.com/TheAhmadOsman/status/2009017277048562086
- **What:** Comprehensive hands-on curriculum for LLM engineering covering tokenization, embeddings, attention mechanisms, transformers, inference optimization, and training techniques with practical project-based learning approach.

## @Dr_Singularity - 40-Hz Sound Therapy for Alzheimer's
> wow
>
> Researchers gave aged rhesus monkeys daily one hour sessions of 40-Hz auditory stimulation (a low-frequency sound) for a week.
>
> In the monkeys, levels of Alzheimer's related Î²-amyloid proteins in the cerebrospinal fluid more than doubled, and this increase persisted for over five weeks after treatment, suggesting that 40-Hz sound stimulation could help the brain clear toxic proteins and might be a promising noninvasive therapy for Alzheimer's disease.

- **Tweet:** https://x.com/Dr_Singularity/status/2009025233387233635
- **What:** Neuroscience research showing that 40-Hz auditory stimulation in aged primates significantly increases clearance of Alzheimer's-related Î²-amyloid proteins, suggesting potential for noninvasive neurological therapy.

## @drwilliamwallac - The brain consumes ~20% of the bodyâ€™s energy (it can be up to 50% around the age
> The brain consumes ~20% of the bodyâ€™s energy (it can be up to 50% around the age of 10).
> 
> Its function, no surprise, depends on consistent metabolism and micronutrient availability
> 
> 1) Homocysteine control
> B6, B9 (folate), B12, riboflavin, choline, and niacin regulate homocysteine. Elevated levels are associated with vascular dysfunction and neurodegeneration.
> 
> 2) Mitochondrial energy metabolism
> B vitamins, lipoic acid, CoQ10, iron, and manganese act as enzyme cofactors in oxidative phosphorylation. Thiamine or riboflavin deficiency directly impairs cerebral ATP generation.
> 3) Neurotransmitter synthesis
> Vitamin B6 is required to synthesize serotonin, dopamine, and GABA from amino acids. Inadequate B6 disrupts excitatoryâ€“inhibitory balance and stress regulation.
> 
> 4) Nerve signal transmission
> DHA, folate, B12, thiamine, and iron support myelin integrity and axonal conduction, enabling efficient action potential propagation.
> 5) Neuronal membrane stability
> DHA and EPA form the structural backbone of neuronal membranes. Vitamins C and E, along with polyphenols, protect these lipids from oxidative damage.
> 
> 6) Neuronal growth and plasticity
> Vitamin D and flavonoids influence gene expression involved in synaptic remodeling, learning, and memory.
> 7) Cerebral blood flow
> Polyphenols improve endothelial function and cerebral perfusion, supporting oxygen and nutrient delivery to active neurons.

- **Tweet:** https://x.com/drwilliamwallac/status/2009031850849714375
- **What:** The brain consumes ~20% of the bodyâ€™s energy (it can be up to 50% around the age

## @alexhillman - Socratic Mode for Effective Claude Code Usage
> 10000% confirmed.
>
> i almost never ask claude code to start with instructions, I ask it to seek out answers and examples, bring them back, present them as options, offer tradeoffs, ask ME questions, etc etc.
>
> by the time we're even PLANNING, all of the useful context is ready.
>
> *Quoting @theodormarcu:* After 6 months of watching engineers use coding agents, I found the most productive users have something in common
>
> The best ones use what I call "Socratic mode"
>
> Instead of just telling the agent what to do, they start with questions that force it to load the right files and actually understand the abstractions. They keep going (and correcting the agent) until they're confident both they and the agent understand the shape of the problem and the goals
>
> The benefit here is that instead of guessing at a plan upfront, you're helping both yourself and the agent truly understand the codebase first before starting to make any changes
>
> By the time you ask it to do something, all the context is already "built" and the path forward is clear

- **Tweet:** https://x.com/alexhillman/status/2009117083645432245
- **Quoted:** https://x.com/theodormarcu/status/2009074224019722424
- **What:** Technique for maximizing Claude Code effectiveness by using exploratory questioning before implementation to build shared context.

## @RituWithAI - Socratic Mode for Code Agents

> When you jump straight to Write Code, the agent is operating on a "cold boot" of understanding. It guesses which files matter. By asking questions first ("Where is the auth logic defined?", "How does X abstract connect to Y?"), you force the model to retrieve and index the relevant dependencies before it tries to generate solutions. It changes the probability distribution of the output from a "Probable Guess" to a "Context-Aware Fact."
> *Replying to @theodormarcu:* After 6 months of watching engineers use coding agents, I found the most productive users have something in common. The best ones use what I call "Socratic mode"...

- **Tweet:** https://x.com/RituWithAI/status/2009152123850874951
- **Parent:** https://x.com/theodormarcu/status/2009074224019722424
- **What:** Rituraj explains Socratic mode for code agentsâ€”asking clarifying questions first before code generation to improve context understanding and output quality. Strategy for effective AI-assisted development.

## @fmfclips - Aerobic Exercise Reverses Brain Aging in Older Adults

> Exercise doesn't just slow brain aging, it can reverse it. In a year-long trial, older adults who did aerobic exercise (~65â€“70% max heart rate, 3x/week for 30 minutes) increased hippocampal volume by about 2%, instead of the usual 1â€“2% yearly shrinkage. This net gain reflects neurogenesis driven in part by higher BDNF, a key protein that supports the growth and survival of new neurons. The same group also showed improvements in learning and memory, underscoring how powerful regular physical activity is for the aging brain.

- **Tweet:** https://x.com/fmfclips/status/2008583243839193239
- **What:** Research demonstrating that moderate aerobic exercise (30 min, 3x/week) can reverse hippocampal volume loss in aging, with improvements in learning and memory tied to elevated BDNF levels.
## @SciTechera - 40-Hz Sound Stimulation Shows Alzheimer's Effects in Aged Monkeys

> Another crazy breakthrough. 40-Hz sound stimulation shows long lasting Alzheimer's effects in aged monkeys. Researchers in China tested whether 40-Hz auditory stimulation could influence Alzheimer's related pathology in aged rhesus monkeys (26â€“31 years old), a primate model closer to humans than mice. The monkeys were exposed to a 1-kHz pure tone modulated at 40 Hz, 1 hour per day for 7 days. After treatment, levels of Î²-amyloid in cerebrospinal fluid (CSF) increased sharply: AÎ²42 rose by ~205% and AÎ²40 by ~201%. About five weeks later, these elevated levels remained stable, suggesting a sustained biological effect rather than a short term response. The researchers interpret the rise in CSF amyloid as a possible sign of enhanced clearance of amyloid from the brain, not increased production. The study supports the idea that non-invasive 40 Hz sensory stimulation could be a low risk, drug free approach to modulating Alzheimer's disease processes, though human trials are still needed to confirm benefit.

- **Tweet:** https://x.com/SciTechera/status/2008589545995202738
- **What:** Research on 40-Hz auditory stimulation as a potential non-invasive treatment for Alzheimer's pathology, with evidence of sustained amyloid clearance effects in aged primates.

## @drwilliamwallac - Master Clock: How the Brain Controls Your Daily Rhythms

> Your body has a master clock hidden deep in the brain, and it quietly controls almost everything you feel during the day. This chart shows what that clock is actually doing, and why your sleep, hunger, hormones, energy, and mood follow the same 24-hour rhythm every single day. Here's the simple breakdown: The "master clock" lives in the "SCN" (as its abbreviated) Located in the hypothalamus, it takes in light from your eyes and uses it to reset your entire system. Light in the morning tells your brain: wake up, raise cortisol, increase alertness. Darkness at night flips the switch: make melatonin, lower body temperature, prepare for sleep. Melatonin rises only when the SCN says it's dark. The pineal gland releases melatonin to start the sleep process. If light hits your eyes at night (phones, TVs, bright LEDs), that signal can shut off or slow down. Serotonin and melatonin are linked. During the day, serotonin helps regulate mood and alertness. At night, the system converts part of that serotonin into melatonin to drive sleep timing. Your organs follow the brain's schedule. Every major organ has its own "clock genes," and they all sync to the SCN. That's why timing matters...

- **Tweet:** https://x.com/drwilliamwallac/status/2008599744663195990
- **What:** Detailed explanation of circadian rhythm physiology, including the suprachiasmatic nucleus (SCN) as the master clock, effects on hormone timing, organ metabolism, and practical recommendations for optimizing circadian alignment.

## @ShiningScience - Aluminum in Brain Tissue and Neurological Disorders

> ðŸ§  People with Alzheimer's, autism, and multiple sclerosis found to have significantly elevated levels of aluminum in their brain.
>
> A compelling new study has revealed a startling trend in the human brain, identifying significantly higher concentrations of aluminum in individuals diagnosed with Alzheimer's disease, autism spectrum disorder, and multiple sclerosis. By comparing brain tissue samples from deceased patients against those of twenty healthy controls, researchers confirmed that aluminum levels were markedly elevated across all these neurodegenerative and neurodevelopmental conditions. The findings underscore a potential environmental factor shared by these diverse diagnoses, raising critical questions about how this common metal interacts with our neural health.

## @dani_avila7 - Why I Disable Claude Code Auto-Compact for Better Context Management

> I've been asked a lot why I keep Claude Code auto-compact turned off. Here's why ðŸ‘‡
>
> If you configure proper context management, commands, sub-agents, and hooks, you almost never hit auto-compact.
>
> Every time it triggered for me, I lost important context. After looking at how compaction actually works, it was clear that key details were being dropped.

- **Tweet:** https://x.com/dani_avila7/status/2008653214472614369
- **Link:** https://code.claude.com/docs/
- **What:** Explanation of why disabling auto-compact in Claude Code is beneficial, with insights on maintaining context through proper configuration and session management.
- **Filed:** [claude-code-auto-compact.md](./knowledge/articles/claude-code-auto-compact.md)

## @mgyarmathy_ - What Makes Ralph Work

> Ralph works because it reflects a few things that good engineers do instinctively:
>
> 1. build a mental plan of small incremental steps
> 2. focus on one step at a time
> 3. construct automated feedback loops to verify changes
> 4. continually re-evaluate progress and revise the plan
>
> *Quoting @mattpocockuk:* There's an AI coding approach that lets you run seriously long-running AI agents (hours, days) that ship code while you sleep. I've tried it, and I'm not going back.
>
> It's the Ralph Wiggum approach. Here it is in a nutshell:
>
> Run a coding agent with a clean slate, again and again until a stop condition is met.
>
> The Bash Script
>
> Running ralph involves a single bash script.
>
> 1. Set up a bash for loop that runs a set number of times, let's say 10. You choose a finite number to prevent the agent running infinitely.
> 2. Inside the loop, you get the coding agent (Claude Code, OpenCode, Codex etc) to work on a single feature in the repo until that single feature is done. You prompt it to say "if, after implementing, there is no further work to be done, reply with <promise>COMPLETE</promise>."
> 3. Check for <promise>COMPLETE</promise> inside the loop and exit early if it exists.
>
> The Stop Condition
>
> How does the LLM know when to emit <promise>COMPLETE</promise>? There are multiple approaches:
>
> - Raw Prompting: Just pass a very clear stop condition to the prompt inside the bash loop. "The job is complete when X conditions are met"
> - TODO list: Give the agent a TODO list to complete
> - PRD: My preferred approach, explained below
>
> Progress Reports
>
> In your prompt, you MUST tell the agent to commit its work, and append its progress to a local progress.txt file.
>
> Committing its work allows future agents to navigate what was done via the git history.
>
> The progress.txt is a standard long-running agent practice. Feed it in to the agent via the prompt, and use the verb 'append' to make sure it doesn't update previous entries.
>
> Keep CI Green
>
> Each commit MUST pass all tests and types. This means you have to prompt the agent to run typechecks and tests on each commit.
>
> If you don't do this, you're hamstringing future agent runs with bad code, and they'll need to bisect to find bugs. Super nasty.
>
> This means that building really healthy feedback loops is CRITICAL to Ralph's success.
>
> The PRD
>
> Two problems immediately emerge with Ralph.
>
> The first is that the agent picks tasks which are too large. They don't scope the amount of work correctly and they try things which are too ambitious. This means they run out of context window and just end up failing.
>
> The second is that the agent doesn't know when to stop.
>
> To solve this, I use a PRD-based approach that formalizes the work I want Ralph to complete in a list of user stories.
>
> It's a mix of a PRD and a TODO-list, where the PRD is a JSON file of user stories with 'passes: false'.
>
> I then prompt the agent to pick the highest priority feature, and ONLY work on that feature. It then updates the passing status of the relevant PRD item at the end.
>
> This scopes it down extremely effectively, meaning it utilizes only a small part of its context window, and thus isn't swamped by context rot.
>
> Summary
>
> - Bash script
> - JSON-based PRD
> - progress.txt
> - Keep CI green
> - Feedback loops
>
> You'll have an AI coding setup that can ship while you kip.

- **Tweet:** https://x.com/mgyarmathy_/status/2008258829860098424
- **Quoted:** https://x.com/mattpocockuk/status/2007924876548637089
- **What:** Quote endorsing Matt Pocock's Ralph Loop approach for long-running AI coding agents, highlighting the core principles of effective incremental development with feedback loops.
